Prophethood

Revelation as an Extra Favor
Refutes that revelation is redundant, arguing it is an extra favor, and classifies bounties into necessities and luxuries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why revelation is considered an additional divine favor beyond reason, and how the classification of blessings into necessities and adornments supports this.

Core Idea
Revelation is not redundant; it is an extra blessing beyond reason.

This passage tackles a key objection against prophethood: if human reason is sufficient for guidance, then sending prophets is unnecessary. The author argues that even if reason were enough, sending messengers is an act of divine generosity—it provides more than the minimum. He illustrates this by showing how Allah's blessings come in two categories: necessities that meet basic needs, and adornments that add delight. Revelation falls into the latter category.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author addresses the claim that revelation is redundant if reason is sufficient. He counters by framing revelation as an extra favor (ifḍāl) and kindness (iḥsān).
The author uses the example of foods with extra pleasure even though basic foods suffice.
2
Understand the analogy of extra pleasure in food
The author argues that giving more than what suffices is generous. He points to foods with added delight beyond mere nourishment—this is analogous to revelation providing more than reason alone.
This analogy shows that extra blessings are not foolish but generous.
3
Learn the classification of divine bounties
The 'wise' (al-ḥukamā') divided blessings into two types: necessities (mirfaq) that fulfill basic needs without extra pleasure (e.g., barley, wool), and adornments (zīna) that include added delight (e.g., tasty foods, fine clothes). Revelation fits the latter category.
This classification directly refutes the claim that revelation is useless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أنه لو كان في العقل الغناء كما يزعمون وليس في إرسال المرسل زيادة تخفيف وتيسير أو معنى من المعاني بل فيه عين ما في العقل، لما أوجب ذلك كون الإرسال سفها، بل هو من باب الإفضال والإحسان، إذ إعطاء ما زاد على الكفاية يعد إفضالاً وزيادة في الإنعام، ووجود ذلك في العالم مما لا يخفى، كإعطاء الله تعالى ما فيه زيادة لذة من الأغذية وإن كان بدون ذلك من الأغذية حصول ما هو المقصود من بقاء الأبدان. ولهذا قسمت الحكماء ما ساق الله تعالى إلى عباده من أنواع نعمه قسمين: أحدهما من لواحق المرفق وهو ما يحصل به المقصود من غير أن يكون فيه زيادة لذة كالشعير والذرة، وغير ذلك من الحبوب التي يحصل بتناولها بقاء الأبدان وكالصوف والشعر اللذين يحصل بهما ما هو المقصود من اللباس من ستر العورة، ودفع أذى الحر والبرد. والآخر من لواحق الزينة وهو ما فيه زيادة لذة وراحة وراء ما يتعلق به من مصلحة البدن وقيام النهمة كالأطعمة المشهية والثياب الفاخرة.
Moreover, if reason were self-sufficient as they claim, and there is no additional easing and facilitation in the sending of messengers, nor any meaning other than what is already in reason, that would not necessitate that sending is foolishness; rather, it is from the chapter of favor and beneficence, since giving what exceeds sufficiency is considered a favor and an increase in kindness. And the existence of that in the world is not hidden, like Allah's giving of what has an increase of pleasure in foods, even though without that food, the intended purpose of preserving bodies is attained. For this reason, the wise divided what Allah sent to His servants from the types of His blessings into two divisions: one of them belongs to the category of necessity (al-mirfaq), which is that by which the intended purpose is attained without there being an increase of pleasure, such as barley and corn, and other grains by which the preservation of bodies is attained, and such as wool and hair by which what is intended from clothing is attained: covering the private parts and repelling the harm of heat and cold. And the other belongs to the category of adornment (al-zīna), which is that in which there is an increase of pleasure and comfort beyond what relates to the benefit of the body and the fulfillment of appetite, such as appetizing foods and fine clothes.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage refutes the objection that revelation is redundant if reason is sufficient. The author argues that even if reason were self-sufficient (as claimed by some), sending messengers would not be foolish; rather, it is an act of favor (ifḍāl) and benevolence (iḥsān). Giving more than what suffices is generous. The author provides an analogy: Allah gives foods with extra pleasure, even though basic foods already preserve life. The wise thus classify divine blessings into two categories: necessities (mirfaq) that meet basic needs without extra pleasure (e.g., barley, wool), and adornments (zīna) that provide added delight (e.g., tasty foods, fine clothes). Revelation falls into the latter category—it is an additional blessing beyond what reason alone provides. Thus, the argument that revelation is unnecessary is refuted.

Key Terms
إفضال
إحسان
مرفق
زينة
كفاية
لذة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If reason is sufficient, then revelation is useless and sending prophets is foolish.
Correction: Revelation is an extra favor beyond reason, just as fine foods and clothes are extra beyond basic necessities—it is not redundant but generous.

Checkpoint
How does the author classify divine blessings to refute the claim that revelation is redundant?
A) Into visible and invisible.
B) Into necessary and excessive.
C) Into necessities (mirfaq) and adornments (zīna).
D) Into physical and spiritual.

Key Takeaway
Revelation is an extra favor from Allah, not a redundancy—just as fine foods and clothes are extra blessings beyond basic necessities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you believe your intellect is enough for guidance, what specific teaching from revelation has changed how you live or think in a way that reason alone never could? Will you thank Allah for that extra favor today?
?
The author compares revelation to luxurious foods and fine clothes. Have you ever treated Allah’s guidance as a mere necessity rather than a delightful gift? What would it look like for you to delight in revelation this week?
?
When you read the Quran or learn the Prophet’s ﷺ teachings, do you experience them as an extra kindness from Allah or a burden? What can you do right now to shift your heart toward gratitude for this favor that surpasses reason?

Thankfulness for Prophethood
The author states that early philosophers were not monotheists except those who followed prophets, and condemns ingratitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the necessity of gratitude for the blessing of prophethood and the author's strong condemnation of those who belittle it.

Core Idea
Ingratitude for prophetic guidance is a sign of incurable foolishness.

The author begins by noting a striking historical fact: among early philosophers, true monotheism was only found in those who followed prophets. He then draws a powerful conclusion: anyone who fails to receive this great blessing with gratitude and instead deems it an unnecessary easement is a fool beyond remedy. This passage establishes the high station of prophethood and the severe consequence of ingratitude.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's observations and conclusion sink in.
Pay attention to the contrast between 'early philosophers' and 'followers of the prophets'.
2
Identify the historical observation
Find the author's claim about the scarcity of monotheists among philosophers. Note his specific criteria: adherence to tawhid, command/prohibition, and belief in resurrection.
This observation sets the stage for the main point.
3
Understand the condemnation
Read the second part. The author describes two responses to prophetic guidance: gratitude or ingratitude. He labels the ingrate as an incurable fool.
Note the strong language: 'no cure' and 'disease of ignorance'.
4
Reflect on the author's rhetoric
Consider why the author uses such harsh terms. This underscores the seriousness of rejecting divine guidance.
Think about what makes prophetic guidance a 'great blessing'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السماوي وخلاء الصنف الثاني عن ذلك، بل لا نكاد نجد أحدًا من الأوائل المنتسبين إلى الفلسفة متمسكاً بالتوحيد مقراً بوظائف الأمر والنهي معترفا بالحشر والمعاد على ما هو الحق إلا إذا كان من أتباع الأنبياء عليهم السلام أخذ الدين منهم أو من أتباعهم، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فمن لم يتلق هذه النعمة العظيمة بالشكر، واعتقد أن الإرشاد إلى ذلك تسهيل الأمر عليه وتخفيف المؤنة خارج عن الحكمة، فهو الأحمق الذي لا دواء لحمقه ولا حيلة لبرئه عما ابتلى به من داء جهله والله الهادي.
The celestial and the emptiness of the second kind from that, rather we scarcely find anyone among the early ones affiliated with philosophy adhering to monotheism, acknowledging the functions of command and prohibition, recognizing the resurrection and the final return as is the truth, except if he was among the followers of the prophets, peace be upon them, taking religion from them or from their followers. And if the matter is so, then whoever does not receive this great blessing with gratitude, and believes that guidance to that is an easing of the matter upon him and a lightening of the burden, outside of wisdom, then he is the fool for whose foolishness there is no cure and no stratagem for his recovery from the disease of ignorance he is afflicted with. And Allah is the Guide.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage opens by observing the historical scarcity of true monotheists among philosophers except those who learned from prophets. It then declares that anyone who fails to appreciate the blessing of prophetic guidance and instead considers it an unnecessary facilitation is foolish beyond remedy. The author emphasizes the greatness of this blessing and the severity of ungratefulness.

Key Terms
توحيد
أنبياء
نعمة
حكمة
حمق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that prophetic guidance is an unnecessary burden or an easing that is not wise.
Correction: The author declares that such a view is foolishness without cure; prophetic guidance is a great blessing that demands gratitude.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about early philosophers?
A) They were all monotheists.
B) They were all polytheists.
C) Few were monotheists except those who followed prophets.
D) They rejected resurrection.

Key Takeaway
The blessing of prophethood demands gratitude; ingratitude is incurable foolishness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the call to prayer or read a prophetic command, does your heart feel gratitude or burden? If the latter, what does this passage reveal about your inner state, and what specific action can you take this week to transform that response into thankfulness?
?
The author says the fool's ignorance is a 'disease' with 'no cure'—yet Allah is the Guide. What area of your life are you currently justifying as 'just my personality' or 'too hard to change'? How does this passage challenge you to seek the cure from Allah through His prophets?
?
Can you recall a specific prophetic teaching you once considered unnecessary or outdated? How does the author's description of the early philosophers' failure without prophets apply to your own reliance on your intellect? What will you commit to learning or obeying today as an act of gratitude?

Even If Reason Sufficed, Sending Prophets Is Beneficence
The author argues that even if reason alone were sufficient, sending prophets would still be an act of beneficence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that sending prophets remains an act of divine beneficence even if reason alone were sufficient for guidance.

Core Idea
Prophecy is a favor, not a necessity – Allah gives beyond what is needed.

In this passage, the author tackles an objection raised by some rationalists: if human reason is enough to know God and moral truths, wouldn't sending prophets be pointless and even foolish? He argues that even in that hypothetical scenario, sending messengers would still be a generous act of God, not a foolish one. He illustrates this with the simple example of food – basic nutrition suffices, but providing tastier food is an extra kindness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Let the author's own words set the context before you analyse.
Notice the phrase 'لو كان في العقل الغناء' – this sets up the conditional.
2
Identify the objection being addressed
The objector says: if reason suffices, then sending prophets is no help and thus foolish. Find where this view is presented.
The phrase 'لما أوجب ذلك كون الإرسال سفها' captures the objection.
3
See the counterargument
The author says it is not foolish but from the category of favor and beneficence. Identify the keywords إفضال and إحسان.
4
Understand the analogy
The author gives an example: Allah provides food that gives extra pleasure beyond mere nutrition. This shows giving beyond necessity is a favor.
Think about how this analogy applies to prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أنه لو كان في العقل الغناء كما يزعمون وليس في إرسال المرسل زيادة تخفيف وتيسير أو معنى من المعاني بل فيه عين ما في العقل، لما أوجب ذلك كون الإرسال سفها، بل هو من باب الإفضال والإحسان، إذ إعطاء ما زاد على الكفاية يعد إفضالاً وزيادة في الإنعام، ووجود ذلك في العالم مما لا يخفى، كإعطاء الله تعالى ما فيه زيادة لذة من الأغذية وإن كان بدون ذلك من الأغذية حصول ما هو المقصود من بقاء الأبدان.
Furthermore, if reason were sufficient as they claim, and in sending the messenger there is no additional relief and facilitation nor any meaning, rather it contains the very same thing that is in reason, that would not necessitate that sending be foolish. Rather, it is from the category of favor and beneficence. For giving what exceeds sufficiency is considered a favor and an increase in bounty. And the existence of that in the world is not hidden, such as Allah's giving what has an increase of pleasure in foods, even if without that from foods the intended purpose of maintaining bodies is attained.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
The author counters the claim that if reason sufficed, sending prophets would be superfluous and foolish. He argues that even in that case, it would still be generous, because giving extra beyond necessity is an act of favor. He gives the example of delicious food: basic nutrition suffices, but providing more pleasure is a bounty.

Key Terms
عقل
إرسال
إفضال
كفاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if reason can guide, prophecy is useless or foolish.
Correction: The author clarifies that even if reason were sufficient, sending prophets is still a gracious act of favor, not foolish.

Checkpoint
According to the author, giving beyond what is needed is considered:
A) Foolishness.
B) Favor and beneficence.
C) Waste.
D) Required.

Key Takeaway
Sending prophets is a divine favor that goes beyond the sufficiency of reason, exemplifying Allah's generosity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you receive a blessing that goes beyond what you need, do you recognize it as a special favor from Allah, or do you take it for granted? How can you train yourself to see Allah's إحسان in the 'extras' of your life? Commit to one specific 'extra' blessing you will thank Allah for today.
?
If you have ever doubted the need for prophets because you think your reason is enough, what does this lesson reveal about Allah's generosity towards you? Will you ask Allah to increase your appreciation for the guidance of the prophets? Make a sincere du'a right now.
?
The analogy of food suggests that Allah gives us more than sustenance. In what area of your life have you been content with 'just enough' spiritually, and how can you seek the 'extra' that Allah offers through prophetic guidance? Write a concrete plan.

Objection and Answer: Sending Prophets to Rejecters
The author presents and answers the objection that sending prophets to rejecters is unwise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that sending prophets to those who reject is unwise, by showing the wisdom in sending to believers and by challenging the analogy from the witnessed to the unseen.

Core Idea
A rejected prophet still brings wisdom to those who accept.

The objection claims that sending a prophet to those who will reject is pointless. The author responds by pointing out that the same act also benefits those who accept, which is wise. He further challenges the assumption that what is unwise in human affairs applies to God's actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection carefully. It claims that sending a prophet to someone who will reject is 'outside wisdom'.
The objection hinges on the idea of foreknowledge.
2
Examine the first response
The author points out that sending prophets also benefits those who accept, which is wise. Notice that the objectors themselves would deny this wisdom.
3
Analyze the second response
The author challenges the analogy from the witnessed (shahid) to the unseen (gha'ib). He argues that the two cases are not necessarily equivalent in meaning.
This is a key principle of kalam: the analogy from shahid to ghā'ib is only valid when the meaning is the same in both realms.
4
Reflect on the argument
Think about why this refutation works. The author uses both a positive argument (benefit to believers) and a logical argument (flawed analogy).
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the core response is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعتراض للخصوم: وما زعموا أن إرسال الرسول إلى من يعلم المرسل أنه لا يجيبه إلى ما يدعوه، ويقابل رسوله بالتكذيب، خارج عن الحكمة. الرد عليه: قلنا لهم وإرسال الرسول إلى من يعلم أنه بجيبه ويقبل نصحه وينجو بذلك عن الهلكة ويكتسب لنفسه به المنافع الجمة ويقبل على طاعة المرسل، حكمة وأنتم تأبون القول به، وتنسبونه أيضا إلى السفه، وهو دليل سفهكم. ثم يقال لهم: إن إرسال الرسول إلى من يعلم أنه لا يجيبه ويوصل المكروه إلى رسوله إن كان في الشاهد قبيحًا فلم قلتم: إنه في الغائب قبيح؟ وبأي معنى تجمعون بينهما؟ وقد بينا غير مرة أن الاستدلال بالشاهد على الغائب إنما يستقيم عند استوائهما في المعنى. ثم تقولون لو أن ملكًا من الملوك علم من طائفة من الناس أنهم لا يطيعونه
Objection of the opponents: And what they claimed that sending the messenger to one whom the sender knows will not respond to what he calls him to, and will meet his messenger with denial, is outside wisdom. The response to it: We say to them: And sending the messenger to one whom he knows will respond to him, accept his advice, and be saved thereby from destruction, and gain for himself abundant benefits, and turn to obedience of the sender, is wisdom, and you refuse to say it, and you also attribute it to foolishness, and that is proof of your foolishness. Then it is said to them: If sending the messenger to one whom he knows will not respond and will bring harm to his messenger is, in the witnessed world, ugly, then why do you say that in the unseen it is ugly? And by what meaning do you compare them? And we have explained many times that inferring from the witnessed to the unseen is only valid when they are equal in meaning. Then you say, if a king knew of a group of people that they would not obey him...
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
The objection claims that sending a prophet who will be rejected is unwise. The author gives a double response: first, sending prophets also benefits those who do accept, which is wise, and the objectors themselves would deny that wisdom. Second, he challenges the analogy from the witnessed world: in the unseen, God's wisdom may differ, and the two cases are not necessarily equivalent. The argument cuts off but the intent is to refute the objection.

Key Terms
اعتراض
حكمة
شاهد
غائب
استدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God sending a prophet to someone who will reject is wasteful and pointless.
Correction: The author shows that sending a prophet also benefits those who accept, which is wise. Additionally, the analogy from human kings to God is flawed because the realms are different.

Checkpoint
How does the author initially respond to the objection?
A) By saying that God does not know who will reject.
B) By saying that the prophet may still benefit those who accept.
C) By saying that rejection does not matter.
D) By saying that the objection is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the objection by highlighting the wisdom in sending prophets for the sake of believers and by calling into question the analogy from human experience to divine action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a verse or hadith that calls you to obedience, do you immediately respond or do you resist? What does your reaction reveal about your trust in Allah's wisdom in sending messengers?
?
Have you ever questioned Allah's wisdom because something didn't make sense to you? How can you train yourself to submit to His wisdom even when you don't see the immediate benefit?
?
The author argues that we cannot judge divine actions by human standards. In what areas of your life do you unconsciously measure Allah's decisions by your own limited understanding? What is one concrete step you can take to submit more fully?

God's Multiple Blessings and Proofs: Generosity, Not Foolishness
Argument that God's multiple acts are kindness not foolishness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the multiplicity of God's proofs and blessings is a sign of generosity and perfection, not foolishness.

Core Idea
Multiple proofs from God are kindness, not foolishness.

This passage by Imam al-Nasafi addresses a common objection: if human reason alone is sufficient to know God, why send prophets and provide multiple proofs? Using analogies from creation, he shows that multiplicity is a form of generosity and completeness, not a defect. As you read, notice how he builds his argument from physical examples to theological conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage aloud if possible. Notice the key terms سفه, إنعام, إفضال, دليل عقلي, غنية وكفاية.
The passage uses repeated patterns: 'لم يعد ذلك سفهًا بل عد...'
2
Analyze the analogies
Identify the three analogies: two eyes/ears, multiple body parts, multiple proofs. How does each analogy support the conclusion?
The analogies move from physical creation to spiritual guidance.
3
Connect to prophethood
See how the author applies the same reasoning to the sending of prophets. What does this tell us about God's wisdom?
The final line mentions that this is a lenient argument; the decisive proof is elsewhere.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بدون ذلك كفاية. لم يعد ذلك سفهًا بل عد إنعامًا وإفضالا وكذا الله تعالى خلق للخلق عينين وأذنين، وكذا كل ذي عدد في الجسم، وإن كانت الحاجة تندفع والمقصود يحصل بواحد ولم يعد ذلك سفهًا بل عد تماما، وكذا الله تعالى لم يكتف بإقامة دليل واحد عقلي على ثبوته وتوحيده، بل جعل في كل مما خلق وذرأ آية لذلك ولم يعد سفها فكذا فيما نحن فيه لم يكن إرسال الرسل سفهًا وإن كان في العقل عنه غنية وكفاية، بل يعد إنعامًا وإفضالاً.
ثم هذا كله منا على طريق المساهلة، فأما دلائل بطلان القول بوقوع الغنية بمجرد العقل فقد سبق ذكرها بعون الله وتوفيقه.
Sufficient without that. That is not considered foolishness but rather kindness and favor. Likewise God, exalted, created for creation two eyes and two ears, and likewise everything possessing a number in the body, even if the need is met and the purpose is achieved by one, and that is not considered foolishness but rather completeness. Likewise God, exalted, did not content Himself with establishing one intellectual proof of His existence and oneness, but placed in every thing He created and scattered a sign of that, and it is not considered foolishness. So likewise in our matter, sending messengers is not foolishness even if there is in the intellect sufficiency and adequacy, but it is considered kindness and favor.
Then all this from us is by way of leniency. As for the proofs of the falsity of the claim of sufficiency by mere intellect, they have been mentioned previously with the help and success of God.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage argues that God's multiple acts (like creating two eyes, two ears, and multiple limbs) and multiple proofs of His existence are not foolishness but kindness and completeness. The author first points out that if one eye or one ear sufficed, having two is not foolish but a perfection. Similarly, if one rational proof suffices for God's existence and unity, having multiple proofs in creation is not foolish. Extending this to the sending of prophets, even if human intellect might theoretically be sufficient, God's sending of many prophets is an act of kindness and favor, not foolishness. Then the author notes that this argument is a lenient one; the actual decisive proofs against the claim that reason alone suffices have already been presented.

Key Terms
سفه
إنعام
إفضال
دليل عقلي
غنية وكفاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Having multiple proofs or blessings implies deficiency or foolishness.
Correction: The passage shows that multiplicity in God's actions is a sign of generosity and perfection, not foolishness.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is sending many prophets not foolish even if reason alone suffices?
A) Because it is a test of faith.
B) Because it is an act of kindness and favor, like multiple limbs and proofs.
C) Because prophets prove their own miracles.
D) Because reason is unreliable.

Key Takeaway
God's multiplicity of proofs and blessings is an act of generosity and completeness, not a sign of foolishness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own body, do you see God's multiple gifts as mere redundancies, or as deliberate acts of generosity that call you to gratitude? How can you train yourself to see every blessing as an invitation to thankfulness?
?
Your mind may tell you that you have enough guidance from logic and nature, yet God keeps sending reminders through prophets and revelation. Do you receive these as bothersome repetitions or as precious favors? What would change if you truly believed each message is a gift?
?
Think of one area in your life where you have doubted God's wisdom in giving you more than what seems necessary—whether in trials, provision, or guidance. Can you now accept that His abundance is not foolishness but love? What commitment will you make today to honor that abundance?

Refuting the Objection of Sending a Prophet to a Non-Responder
Objection and answer regarding prophet sent to non-responder

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi theologians refute the objection that sending a prophet to a non-responder is unwise, using the distinction between visible and invisible realms.

Core Idea
God's wisdom is not limited by human analogies from the visible world.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi addresses a common objection: if God knows someone will reject a prophet, why send one? The objectors claim this is unwise. The response reveals a key principle of kalam: we cannot judge the divine realm by human standards alone. Let's examine the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two parts: the objection (what they claimed) and the author's reply (we said, then it is said).
2
Identify the two arguments
The author gives two refutations: first, a tu quoque about the opponent's inconsistency; second, a methodological point about analogy from the visible to the invisible.
The tu quoque argument shows the opponents contradict themselves.
3
Reflect on the principle of istidlal
The author concludes that inference from visible to invisible works only when the meanings are equivalent. Ask: why is this important for theology?
This principle protects God's transcendence from anthropomorphic reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما زعموا أن إرسال الرسول إلى من يعلم المرسل أنه لا يجيبه إلى ما يدعوه، ويقابل رسوله بالتكذيب، خارج عن الحكمة.
قلنا لهم وإرسال الرسول إلى من يعلم أنه يجيبه ويقبل نصحه وينجو بذلك عن الهلكة ويكتسب لنفسه به المنافع الجمة ويقبل على طاعة المرسل، حكمة وأنتم تأبون القول به، وتنسبونه أيضا إلى السفه، وهو دليل سفهكم.
ثم يقال لهم : إن إرسال الرسول إلى من يعلم أنه لا يجيبه ويوصل المكروه إلى رسوله إن كان في الشاهد قبيحًا فلم قلتم : إنه في الغائب قبيح؟ وبأي معنى تجمعون بينهما؟ وقد بينا غير مرة أن الاستدلال بالشاهد على الغائب إنما يستقيم عند استوائهما في المعنى.
And what they claimed that sending the messenger to one whom the sender knows will not respond to what he calls him to, and will meet his messenger with denial, is outside of wisdom.
We said to them: And sending the messenger to one whom He knows will respond to him and accept his advice and thereby be saved from destruction and acquire for himself abundant benefits and accept the obedience of the sender, is wisdom, and you refuse to say that, and you also attribute it to foolishness, and that is evidence of your foolishness.
Then it is said to them: If sending the messenger to one whom He knows will not respond and will bring harm to his messenger is ugly in the visible world, why do you say it is ugly in the invisible? And in what sense do you equate them? And we have stated more than once that inference from the visible to the invisible is only valid when they are equivalent in meaning.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from opponents: sending a prophet to someone whom God knows will not respond and will deny the messenger is outside wisdom. The author answers in two ways. First, if sending a prophet to a responder is wisdom (which the opponents also reject, showing their own foolishness), then by their own logic they contradict. Second, the author questions the analogy between the visible and invisible realms. Even if sending a messenger to a known rejector is considered ugly in human affairs, why assume it is ugly in God's affairs? Inference from visible to invisible is valid only when the meanings are equivalent, which is not the case here.

Key Terms
إرسال الرسول
حكمة
سفه
الشاهد
الغائب
استدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's sending a prophet to a non-responder is futile because He knows the outcome.
Correction: God's actions are not bound by human standards of utility; the act has multiple wisdoms beyond human comprehension.

Checkpoint
What is the main flaw the author points out in the objection about sending a prophet to a non-responder?
A) The objection assumes that God does not know the future.
B) The objection makes an invalid analogy between the visible and invisible.
C) The objection ignores that the prophet might convert later.
D) The objection denies that God is wise.

Key Takeaway
The objection that sending a prophet to a non-responder is unwise is refuted by showing that human analogies cannot be directly applied to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you judged God's actions—perhaps a trial or unanswered prayer—as 'unwise' because it didn't fit your understanding? How does this passage expose the pride behind that judgment, and what would it look like for you to surrender that area to His infinite wisdom today?
?
The opponents based their argument on visible-world logic. In what specific areas of your life are you still operating as if God must conform to human reasoning? Will you consciously choose to trust that His ways are higher, even when they don't make sense to you?
?
If inference from the visible to the invisible is invalid unless equivalent, what does that mean for how you think about God's attributes? Are there ways you have unknowingly reduced God to human proportions? How can you cultivate a deeper reverence for His transcendence starting now?

The King's Wise Strategy: Defending Prophethood
Analogy of a king sending messengers to rebels to prove wisdom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the analogy of a king sending messengers to rebels demonstrates that sending prophets is wise, even if the recipients are known to reject.

Core Idea
Establishing an argument before punishment is wise, not foolish.

This passage uses a powerful analogy to refute the claim that sending a prophet to those who will reject is unwise. By comparing God to a wise king, the author shows that sending messengers actually establishes justice and wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the king knows the outcome, yet he still sends messengers.
Pay attention to why the king does this.
2
Identify the opponent's objection
The passage begins with 'you say' — this is the opponent's objection. Understand that punishing immediately would be seen as unjust.
3
Trace the king's reasoning
The king sends messengers to establish his argument and to reward the messenger. This makes his action appear wise, not foolish.
The author then asks the opponent to judge.
4
Apply the conclusion
If the opponent says the king is wise, then God's sending prophets is also wise. If they say the king is unwise, they show ignorance.
This forces the opponent to admit their mistake.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم تقولون لو أن ملكًا من الملوك علم من طائفة من الناس أنهم لا يطيعونه ولا ينقادون له بل يؤثرون عداوته، وينتصبون حربًا لأوليائه، وعلم أنه لو قابلهم بالاستئصال وعاملهم بالتنكيل والاجتياح قبل الأعداد والأنداد، وأخذ الحجة عليهم، لنسبه من عاين فعله أو بلغه صنيعه إلى الظلم والعدوان، ووسمه بسمة الجور والظلم والبغي، فبعث إليهم سفراء وقدم إليهم بشراء ونذراء ليلزمهم حجته ويظهر لدى الناس في الانتقام منهم وإنزال البأس أنه يعطى الرسول بمقابلة ما يصل إليه من المكروه الذي ينقضي في أسرع مدة نعما دائمة يتقلب فيها مدة مديدة وينيله بمقابلة ذلك من اللذات ما يتمنى الرسول أن لو كان أوصل إليه من المكروه أضعاف ما أوصل، أكان هذا المرسل حكيمًا في ذلك؟ فإن قالوا: لا، ظهر جهلهم بالحكمة والسفه، وإن قالوا: نعم، ظهر أن ما ظنوه سفها، حكمة، وخاب سعيهم وبطلت شبهتهم، وبالله التوفيق.
Then you say: If a king among kings knew of a group of people that they would not obey him and would not submit to him, but rather prefer his enmity, and set themselves up for war against his allies, and he knew that if he were to annihilate them and treat them with punishment and destruction before rivals and opponents, and take the argument against them, whoever witnessed his action or heard of his deed would attribute to him oppression and aggression, and brand him with the mark of injustice and oppression and transgression; so he sent to them ambassadors and presented to them bringers of good news and warners to establish his argument against them and to make apparent to the people in taking revenge on them and inflicting punishment that he gives the messenger, in exchange for what reaches him of harm that passes in the fastest time, lasting blessings in which he rolls for a long period, and gives him in exchange for that of pleasures such that the messenger wishes that he had brought upon himself double of what he brought. Was this sender wise in that? If they say no, their ignorance of wisdom and foolishness becomes apparent. If they say yes, it becomes apparent that what they considered foolishness is wisdom, and their effort failed and their doubt was invalidated. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed analogy to refute the claim that sending a prophet to a known rejector is unwise. The author describes a king who knows a group will disobey and fight him. If he were to punish them immediately, he would be seen as unjust. Instead, he sends messengers with warnings to establish his argument, and he promises the messenger great rewards for enduring temporary harm. The king's action is wise because it establishes the argument and compensates the messenger. The author then forces the opponent: if they say such a king is not wise, they show ignorance; if they say he is wise, then they must admit that God's sending prophets is also wise, refuting their original objection.

Key Terms
ملك
حكمة
سفه
حجة
رسول
عقاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Punishing rejectors immediately is wise.
Correction: The passage shows that establishing the argument first is wiser, as it prevents accusations of injustice.

Checkpoint
In the king analogy, why does the king send messengers despite knowing the people will reject?
A) To give them a chance to repent.
B) To establish his argument before punishing, and to reward the messenger.
C) To make an example of them.
D) Because he hopes they will change.

Key Takeaway
The king's action of sending messengers despite known rejection shows that establishing an argument before punishment is wise, thereby defending God's sending of prophets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear warnings from God's messengers, do you respond like the rebels who 'prefer his enmity' and 'set themselves up for war'? Or do you humble yourself before the One who sends them? What would it take for you to truly listen today?
?

?
Do you sometimes think God is slow or weak because He does not punish disbelief immediately? How does the king's strategy in this analogy correct that misconception in your heart? Will you now trust His wisdom over your impatience?

Refuting the Claim That Divine Commands Are Foolish
Response to al-Khali' on command/prohibition being foolish

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine commands and prohibitions are not foolish but are part of a wise purpose that gives meaning to creation.

Core Idea
Divine obligation transforms creation from meaningless to meaningful.

Al-Khali' argued that God's commands are foolish if they don't benefit God. The author responds by showing that without the system of reward and punishment, creation would indeed be purposeless. But because God has established obligation and an afterlife, creation is wise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical structure: objection, hypothetical, conclusion, rebuttal.
2
Identify al-Khali's objection
Find the claim that al-Khali' made about commands and prohibitions. What is the basis of his argument?
He says that commanding without benefit for the commander is foolish.
3
Understand the counterargument
The author uses a hypothetical scenario: what if there were no obligation, reward, or punishment? Then creation would be purposeless. But since God has established these, creation is wise.
The key move is showing that taklif gives purpose to existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد خرج الجواب عما تعلق به الخليع، أن الأمر والنهي بما لا نفع للأمر في تحصيله ولا ضرر على الناهي في فعل ما نهى عنه سفه، إذ قد بينا في أثناء كلامنا أن التكليف لو لم يكن وانعدم الثواب والعقاب ولم تصر الدنيا مطية إلى الآخرة التي فيها يجازى المحسن بإحسانه والمسيء بإساءته، صار تخليقه العالم ليوجد ثم يتلاشى ويثبت ثم ينعدم، ويفنى من غير أن يتعلق به غرض أو يحصل له عاقبة حميدة. وهذا النوع من الفعل سفه يذم فاعله، وتأبى الحكمة إطلاقه، وذا غير جائز على الله تعالى.
فإذا صار تخليق العالم بواسطة التكليف حكمة، فمن ظن ذلك سفها فهو سفه.
And the answer has emerged to what al-Khali' clung to, that commanding and prohibiting what no benefit for the commander in its attainment nor harm on the prohibitor in doing what he has forbidden is foolishness, since we have explained in the course of our words that if there were no obligation and reward and punishment were absent, and this world did not become a mount to the afterlife in which the good-doer is rewarded for his good and the evildoer for his evil, then His creation of the world to come into being then vanish and be established then cease to exist, and perish without any purpose being attached to it or a praiseworthy outcome obtained for it. And this type of action is foolishness, its doer is blamed, and wisdom refuses to release it, and that is not permissible for God, exalted.
So when the creation of the world by means of obligation became wisdom, then whoever thinks that is foolishness, he is foolish.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection of al-Khali', who claimed that God's commands and prohibitions are foolish if they bring no benefit to God or harm to Him. The author responds that without divine obligation (taklif), reward, and punishment, and without this world serving as a means to the afterlife, the creation of the world would be purposeless—coming into existence only to perish without a beneficial outcome. Such an act would be foolish and blameworthy in wisdom, and cannot be attributed to God. However, because God has established obligation and reward, the creation of the world becomes wise. Therefore, anyone who still considers these as foolish is themselves foolish.

Key Terms
أمر
نهي
سفه
تكليف
ثواب
عقاب
حكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's commands are for His own benefit.
Correction: The passage clarifies that commands serve to test humans for afterlife reward, not to benefit God.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is God's command and prohibition not foolish?
A) Because they benefit God.
B) Because they are part of the test that gives purpose to creation through reward and punishment.
C) Because they are arbitrary.
D) Because they prevent evil.

Key Takeaway
Divine commands and prohibitions are wise because they give creation purpose through obligation, reward, and punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if this world is a mount to the afterlife, or do you sometimes act as if it is an end in itself? What would change if you truly believed every action is a test?
?
Have you ever felt that some of God's commands seem foolish to you? How does the argument in this passage challenge that feeling, and what would it mean for you to fully submit to His wisdom?
?
If you are not living with the awareness of reward and punishment, are you not, in effect, treating creation as meaningless? What step can you take today to align your actions with the purpose of obligation?

The Analogy of the Wise Sender: Refuting the Charge of Folly
Analogy refuting the charge of divine folly through a king's wise action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the analogy of the king sending messengers refutes the claim that God's commands are foolish by demonstrating that apparent harm is compensated by eternal reward, making the sender wise.

Core Idea
Divine commands that seem harsh are actually wise because they lead to eternal bliss.

In this passage, the author presents a powerful analogy to counter the accusation that God's commands are senseless. He describes a king who, instead of annihilating his enemies, sends messengers with warnings and rewards them generously for any harm they endure. The analogy challenges us to recognize that wisdom is not measured by immediate outcomes but by ultimate consequences. Let's explore how this argument dismantles the charge of folly.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy scenario
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the scenario of the king and his enemies.
2
Identify the king's two options
Notice the two courses of action: immediate punishment versus sending messengers with rewards.
3
Analyze the rhetorical question
Consider the two possible answers to the question 'Is this sender wise?' and what each reveals about the opponent's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا ينقادون له بل يؤثرون عداوته، وينتصبون حربًا لأوليائه، وعلم أنه لو قابلهم بالاستئصال وعاملهم بالتنكيل والاجتياح قبل الأعداد والأنداد، وأخذ الحجة عليهم، لنسبه من عاين فعله أو بلغه صنيعه إلى الظلم والعدوان، ووسمه بسمة الجور والظلم والبغي، فبعث إليهم سفراء وقدم إليهم بشراء ونذراء ليلزمهم حجته ويظهر لدى الناس في الانتقام منهم وإنزال البأس أنه يعطى الرسول بمقابلة ما يصل إليه من المكروه الذي ينقضي في أسرع مدة نعما دائمة يتقلب فيها مدة مديدة وينيله بمقابلة ذلك من اللذات ما يتمنى الرسول أن لو كان أوصل إليه من المكروه أضعاف ما أوصل، أكان هذا المرسل حكيمًا في ذلك؟ فإن قالوا: لا، ظهر جهلهم بالحكمة والسفه، وإن قالوا: نعم، ظهر أن ما ظنوه سفها، حكمة، وخاب سعيهم وبطلت شبهتهم، وبالله التوفيق.
And they do not submit to him, but rather prefer his enmity and set themselves up as warriors against his allies. And he knew that if he were to annihilate them and treat them with punishment and extermination before (addressing) the numbers and rivals, and take the argument against them, then whoever witnessed his deed or was informed of his action would attribute to him injustice and aggression, and brand him with the mark of tyranny and oppression and transgression. Is this sender wise in that? If they say: No, their ignorance of wisdom and folly becomes apparent. And if they say: Yes, it becomes apparent that what they considered folly is wisdom, and their effort fails and their false argument is nullified. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage presents a hypothetical analogy to refute the claim that divine commands are foolish. The author describes a situation where a powerful king's enemies refuse to submit. If the king immediately destroyed them, he would be accused of injustice. Instead, he sends emissaries and warners, then rewards the messenger for temporary harm with eternal bliss. The author then asks whether this sender is wise. If the opponent says no, they reveal ignorance; if yes, their claim that such actions are foolish is refuted. The analogy demonstrates that God's sending of prophets and giving of commands, despite initial hardship, is wise due to the eternal reward.

Sending Messengers: Possible and Wajib According to the Muhaqqiqun
Affirms possibility and wisdom-based necessity of sending messengers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi scholars affirm both the logical possibility and the wisdom-based necessity (wujub) of sending prophets.

Core Idea
Sending messengers is possible and, for Maturidis, required by divine wisdom.

This lesson examines a foundational Maturidi position on prophethood. The author first establishes that sending prophets is logically possible (not impossible), then explains that for the verified scholars (muhaqqiqun) it is a necessary requirement (wajib) stemming from God's attribute of wisdom. This is a nuanced position that avoids both outright denial of obligation and the Mu'tazili idea of obligation based on what is most beneficial.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author makes two distinct claims: one about possibility, one about obligation. The passage is incomplete but captures the core reasoning.
The text ends mid-sentence, but the main point is clear.
2
Distinguish between possibility and obligation
The author first says sending messengers is in the realm of possibilities (mumkinat), meaning no logical impossibility. Then he says according to the muhaqqiqun it is in the realm of obligations (wajibat) as a requirement of divine wisdom. Understand that this obligation is not external to God but flows from His attributes.
The key term 'مقتضيات حكمه الحكيم' means 'requirements of His wise judgment.'
3
Reflect on the meaning of 'wajib' here
The author clarifies that this necessity is derived from God's wisdom, not from any external compulsion. Maturidis affirm that God does what is wise and fitting, so sending messengers is a necessary consequence of His wisdom, not a legal obligation imposed by another.
This distinguishes the Maturidi position from the Mu'tazili view that God must do what is most beneficial for creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت أن إرسال الرسل من الله تعالى إلى عباده في حيز الممكنات وأنه لا امتناع له. إرسال الرسل من مقتضيات حكمه الحكيم ثم عند المحققين من متكلمي أصحابنا هو في حيز الواجبات أعني أنه من مقتضيات حكمة الحكيم جل وعلا على نحو ما بينت، ثم على قول هؤلاء وإن كان
And when it is established that sending messengers from God Almighty to His servants is in the realm of possibilities and that there is no impossibility for it. Sending messengers is among the requirements of His wise judgment. Then according to the verified scholars among our companions, it is in the realm of obligations—I mean that it is among the requirements of the wisdom of the Wise, Exalted and Glorious, in the manner I have explained. Then according to the statement of these, even if...
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage affirms two points: first, that sending prophets is logically possible, not impossible. Second, for the verified scholars (muhaqqiqun) of the Maturidi school, it is in the realm of obligations (wajibat), meaning it is a necessary consequence of God's wisdom. The author clarifies that this type of obligation is not like the Mu'tazili obligation based on what is most beneficial, but rather stems from God's attribute of wisdom. The fragment at the end suggests a further distinction between different positions within the school, but the page cuts off.

Correction: The passage clarifies that the muhaqqiqun affirm a type of obligation derived from divine wisdom, not from external imposition. Sending messengers is necessary because God's wisdom demands it, not because any outside force compels Him.

Refuting the Mu'tazila on Obligatory Prophethood
Al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila doctrine that God must send prophets based on the principle of 'اصلح'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument for obligatory prophethood based on 'اصلح' and Al-Nasafi's refutation.

Core Idea
God is not obligated to do what is most beneficial for creation.

Prophetology is central to kalam, but why must God send prophets? The Mu'tazila argued it is obligatory because it is most beneficial for humanity. In this passage, Al-Nasafi declares this false and warns Maturidi theologians not to adopt the principle of 'اصلح'. Let's see the argument and its response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation to get the author's words directly.
Notice the compact style of argumentation.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
Find the two parts of their argument: sending prophets is most beneficial, and what is most beneficial is obligatory upon God.
3
Understand Al-Nasafi's response
He calls it false speech and refers to a later refutation. Notice he doesn't refute it here but sets the stage.
The full refutation comes when discussing 'اصلح' in detail.
4
Apply the warning
Consider why a Maturidi theologian must avoid the اصلح principle when proving prophethood. This shows the danger of adopting a flawed premise.
The Maturidi position on necessity is based on divine wisdom, not obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما المعتزلة فإنهم يقولون بوجوب إرسال الرسل، لما أن ذلك أصلح للعباد وما هو الأصلح للعباد فهو واجب التحصيل على الله تعالى وذلك كلام فاسد نبين فساده عند بلوغنا إلى إبطال القول بالأصلح، فينبغي لمن يتكلم من أصحابنا في هذه المسألة أن يكون بصيرًا بذلك ليصون نفسه في إثبات الرسالة عن الوقوع في القول بالأصلح، والله الموفق.
As for the Mu'tazila, they say that sending prophets is obligatory, because that is most beneficial (اصلح) for servants, and what is most beneficial for servants is obligatory for God to accomplish. That is false speech, whose falsity we will show when we reach the refutation of the doctrine of 'اصلح'. It is therefore incumbent on whoever from our companions speaks on this matter to be knowledgeable about that, so as to protect himself in proving prophethood from falling into the doctrine of 'اصلح'. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage addresses the Mu'tazila position on the necessity of sending prophets. The Mu'tazila argue that God must send prophets because it is most beneficial (اصلح) for human beings, and whatever is most beneficial for them is obligatory upon God to bring about. Al-Nasafi declares this statement false (كلام فاسد) and notes that its falsity will be demonstrated when the doctrine of اصلح is refuted later in the work. He advises Maturidi theologians to be cautious not to inadvertently adopt the اصلح principle while arguing for the necessity of prophethood. This reveals the importance of maintaining the Maturidi position that God's actions are not subject to obligation based on human notions of benefit.

Misconception: The Maturidi view that sending prophets is necessary is the same as the Mu'tazila view.
Correction: The Maturidi view is based on divine wisdom, not on an obligation upon God to do what is most beneficial for creation.

B) Logical necessity
C) It is most beneficial (اصلح) for servants

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that sending prophets is obligatory because it is most beneficial for servants, which Al-Nasafi rejects as false and warns against adopting the اصلح principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Al-Nasafi warns against falling into the اصلح principle. Have you ever judged God's actions based on your limited understanding of what is good? Repent and affirm that God's wisdom surpasses your own.

Why a Prophet Must Bring Proof
Al-Nasafi argues that prophethood's general necessity does not exempt a claimant from providing proof of appointment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi argues that even if prophethood is generally necessary, each claimant must still provide a specific proof, and how this refutes the Ibadiyya.

Core Idea
General necessity does not imply specific identity; each claimant needs a proof.

Al-Nasafi builds on the earlier discussion of prophethood's necessity. He clarifies that while sending prophets may be required by divine wisdom, this does not automatically validate any individual who claims to be a prophet. In fact, the specific appointment of a claimant remains a contingent matter, requiring its own evidence. This principle is then used to refute the Ibadiyya sect, who insisted on immediate acceptance without proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice how al-Nasafi moves from the general principle to the specific requirement and then to the refutation.
Pay attention to the distinction between general necessity and specific appointment.
2
Understand the distinction
Identify the two levels: (1) prophethood as generally necessary according to the investigators, and (2) the specification for a particular person as a possibility. Why does this distinction matter?
The distinction is crucial because it shows that necessity at one level does not transfer to another.
3
Follow the refutation
See how al-Nasafi uses this argument to refute the Ibadiyya who claim that a prophet's mere word must be accepted without proof.
The refutation is based on the principle that contingent things require proof.
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider the underlying principle: contingent matters require proof. How does this apply to other theological claims?
This principle is foundational in kalam for establishing truth claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت أن إرسال الرسل من الله تعالى إلى عباده في حيز الممكنات وأنه لا امتناع له. إرسال الرسل من مقتضيات حكمه الحكيم] ثم عند المحققين من متكلمي أصحابنا هو في حيز الواجبات أعني أنه من مقتضيات حكمة الحكيم جل وعلا على نحو ما بينت، ثم على قول هؤلاء وإن كان هو في نفسه واجب الوجود على ما قررنا، فهو في حق كل فرد من أفراد الناس زمان جواز ورود الرسول وتعين هذا المدعي له في حيز الممكنات أيضا، إذ الدليل عند القائلين بوجوبه إن دل على ثبوته لا محالة، فلا دليل يدل على ثبوته في حق هذا الشخص المتعين، فلابد إذا لكل مدَّع يدعي ذلك في حق نفسه من إقامة دليل يدل على تعينه لذلك، لأن تعينه له من جملة الممكنات فلا يثبت إلا بدليل يوجب ثبوته. وبهذا يعرف بطلان قول الإباضية من الخوارج أن مجرد قول الرسول واجب القبول وأن تعرى عن الدليل، وأن الراد عليه يكفر في ساعته. إذ لا ثبوت لما جاز عليه العدم إلا بدليل يوجب ثبوته.
And if it is established that sending prophets from God to His servants is in the realm of possibilities and that there is no impossibility to it. [Sending prophets is among the requisites of His wise judgment.] Then according to the investigators (المحققين) among the theologians of our companions, it is in the realm of obligations, meaning that it is among the requisites of the wisdom of the Wise, Exalted and High, in the manner I have explained. Then according to these, even if it is in itself necessary in existence (واجب الوجود) as we have established, it is, with respect to every individual among people at the time of the possibility of the arrival of a messenger, and the specification of this claimant for it, also in the realm of possibilities. Because the evidence according to those who affirm its obligation, if it indicates its establishment without doubt, there is no evidence that indicates its establishment with respect to this specific person. Therefore, for every claimant who claims this for himself, it is necessary to produce evidence that indicates his specification for it. Because his being specified for it is among the realm of possibilities, so it is only established by evidence that necessitates its establishment. And by this is known the falsity of the statement of the Ibadiyya from the Khawarij that the mere speech of a messenger is obligatory to accept, even if it is devoid of evidence, and that the one who rejects it is an unbeliever immediately. For there is no establishment for that which admits non-existence except by evidence that necessitates its establishment.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage presents a nuanced argument about the proof of prophethood. First, al-Nasafi states that sending prophets is established as possible (ممكن). Then he notes that according to the investigators (المحققون) among the Maturidi theologians, sending prophets is in the realm of obligations (واجبات) from the perspective of divine wisdom — meaning it is required by God's wisdom, not that it is obligatory upon God. Even so, he argues that the general necessity of prophethood does not automatically apply to any specific individual claiming to be a prophet. For each individual, the specific appointment (تعين) is a matter of possibility (ممكن), because it is possible that this person is a prophet or not. Therefore, a claimant must bring a specific proof (دليل) to show that he is indeed appointed. The author then refutes the Ibadiyya sect who assert that a claimant's mere statement is sufficient and must be accepted without evidence, and that anyone who rejects it is immediately an unbeliever. Al-Nasafi declares this false because anything that could be non-existent (i.e., contingent) requires a proof to establish its existence.

Key Terms
المحققون
تعين
ممكنات
الإباضية
الخوارج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if prophethood is necessary by wisdom, then no proof is needed for a claimant.
Correction: The author clarifies that the general necessity does not entail specific identity; each claimant needs a proof because his appointment is contingent.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Nasafi say that a claimant to prophethood must bring a specific proof?
A) Because prophethood is impossible
B) Because the specific appointment is a matter of possibility (mumkin)
C) Because the Mu'tazila require it
D) Because the Ibadiyya reject proofs

Key Takeaway
A claim to prophethood, even if prophethood is necessary, still requires a specific proof because appointment is contingent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson insists that even prophethood’s general necessity does not let a claimant off the hook: they must bring proof of their appointment. In your own life, do you ever accept religious claims without asking for the specific evidence that the person is genuinely sent by God? What would change if you applied this standard to every teacher, preacher, or influencer you follow?
?
The Ibadiyya believed that rejecting a claimant without evidence was immediate disbelief. How does this warning against blind acceptance challenge your own reactions when you hear a controversial religious claim? Are you quick to dismiss or quick to accept without proof? What commitment can you make today to examine claims with the same rigor al-Nasafi demands?
?
If you were to stand before God and be asked why you believed in His messenger, could you point to the specific proofs that convinced you? This lesson shows that faith should rest on evidence—have you ever examined the proofs of prophethood for yourself, or do you simply inherit belief? What step can you take this week to deepen your certitude?

Definition of Miracle and Its Basis
Al-Nasafi defines the miracle and its basis in incapacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the basis and definition of a miracle (معجزة) as the proof of prophethood according to al-Nasafi.

Core Idea
A miracle proves prophethood by showing the incapacity of others to match it.

Al-Nasafi begins by introducing the miracle as the evidence for prophethood. He emphasizes that to understand the miracle, we must first grasp its basis: the concept of incapacity (عجز). This passage lays the foundation for how miracles function as signs of truthfulness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the key term 'معجزة' and its relation to 'عجز'.
The passage includes a subheading [مأخذ المعجزة] which marks the start of the explanation.
2
Identify the basis of the miracle
Find where al-Nasafi states the basis (مأخذ) of the miracle. What is it? How does it relate to the term 'معجزة'?
The basis is 'العجز' (incapacity), the opposite of 'قدرة' (ability).
3
Reflect on the purpose of the miracle
Consider why the miracle is called 'معجزة'—because it shows the incapacity of those challenged. How does this indicate the truthfulness of the prophet?
The miracle serves as a sign that only God could enable such an act, affirming the prophet's truthfulness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الدليل يسمى معجزة وهو في نفسه ينقسم إلى قسمين فيحتاج إلى القول في مأخذها وحدها وبيان قسميها ووجه كيفية دلالتها على صدق الآتي بها. [مأخذ المعجزة]: فأما مأخذها فهو العجز الذي هو نقيض القدرة سميت معجزة لأنها تظهر عجز من يتحدى به عن معارضتها
Then the evidence is called a miracle (معجزة), and in itself it is divided into two divisions. So it is necessary to speak about its basis (مأخذ) and its definition (حدها), and an explanation of its two divisions, and the way of its indication of the truthfulness of the one who brings it. [The basis of the miracle]: As for its basis, it is incapacity (عجز), which is the opposite of ability (قدرة). It is called a miracle because it shows the incapacity of the one who is challenged by it to oppose it.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage introduces the concept of the miracle (معجزة) as the proof of prophethood. The author first states that the evidence (الدليل) for prophethood is called a miracle. He notes that the miracle itself is divided into two divisions (قسمين) — though the divisions are not elaborated in this passage — and that one must discuss its basis (مأخذ), its definition (حدها), its two divisions, and how it indicates the truthfulness of the prophet. Then he focuses on the basis: the miracle is based on the concept of incapacity (عجز), which is the opposite of ability (قدرة). The term 'miracle' (معجزة) is derived from 'عجز' because it demonstrates that those challenged to produce something similar are incapable of doing so. Thus, the miracle serves as a sign that the prophet is truthful, as only God can enable such an act that others cannot replicate.

Key Terms
معجزة
عجز
قدرة
دليل
صدق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A miracle is simply a supernatural act without any challenge.
Correction: Al-Nasafi defines a miracle as an act that demonstrates the incapacity of the challenged opponent, thereby proving the prophet's truthfulness.

Checkpoint

A) Power (قدرة)
B) Knowledge (علم)
C) Incapacity (عجز)
D) Divine command

Key Takeaway
A miracle is defined by its basis in showing the incapacity of challengers, thus serving as proof of prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If the basis of a miracle is the exposure of human inability, what does that say about your own reliance on your abilities? Are you willing to admit your weakness and turn to the One who sent the miracles?

The Linguistic Root of Mu'jiza: 'Ajz and Its Derivation
Explains the linguistic origin of the word mu'jiza as evidence for prophethood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term mu'jiza is derived from 'ajz (incapacity), its metaphorical application, and the intensifying role of the هاء suffix.

Core Idea
The word mu'jiza comes from 'ajz (incapacity) — it exposes the opponent's inability.

Before we can discuss the two divisions of miracles and how they prove prophethood, the author first clarifies the linguistic basis of the term mu'jiza. Understanding its root ('ajz) and the grammatical features like the intensifying هاء helps us appreciate why a miracle is called 'that which incapacitates.' This passage gives us the necessary vocabulary for the entire discussion of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the basic meaning of the terms and the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the root word 'ajz and the suffix ha.
2
Identify the root 'ajz
The author states that the ma'khadh (root) of mu'jiza is 'ajz, the opposite of qudra. Understand why it is called a miracle: because it manifests the incapacity of the one challenged.
3
Analyze the metaphorical usage and suffix
Note that the term is applied metaphorically to the one who manifests incapacity. Then examine the هاء suffix for exaggeration, similar to words like 'allāma. This shows the emphasis on the opponents' complete incapacity.
Reflect on how the linguistic details reinforce the theological purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الدليل يسمى معجزة وهو في نفسه ينقسم إلى قسمين فيحتاج إلى القول في مأخذها وحدها وبيان قسميها ووجه كيفية دلالتها على صدق الآتي بها. فأما مأخذها فهو العجز الذي هو نقيض القدرة سميت معجزة لأنها تظهر عجز من يتحدى به عن معارضتها. ثم المعجز في الحقيقة وإن كان اسماً لمثبت العجز كالمقدر اسم لمثبت القدرة إلا أن المظهر للعجز يسمى به مجازا، ثم الهاء الداخلة في لفظها للمبالغة، كما في العلامة والنسابة والراوية، فكانت داخلة فيها للمبالغة في الخبر عن عجز المرسل إليهم.
Then the evidence is called a miracle (mu'jiza), and it in itself is divided into two divisions, so there is need to discuss its root and its definition and to explain its two divisions and the manner of its indication of the truthfulness of the one who brings it. As for its root, it is the incapacity ('ajz) which is the opposite of capability. It was called a miracle because it manifests the incapacity of the one who is challenged to oppose it. Then the miracle in reality, even though it is a name for something that establishes incapacity, just as 'muqaddir' is a name for something that establishes capability, yet the one who manifests incapacity is called by it metaphorically. Then the 'hā' entered into its word for exaggeration, as in 'al-‘allāma', 'al-nassāba', and 'al-rāwiya'. Thus it was entered into it for exaggeration in reporting the incapacity of those to whom the messenger was sent.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage explains the term 'mu'jiza' as the evidence for prophethood, derived from 'ajz (incapacity). The miracle is so named because it shows the incapacity of the challenged person to produce its like. The derivation is metaphorical: the miracle itself establishes incapacity, but the word is applied to the act. The 'hā' suffix indicates exaggeration, similar to words like 'allāma (great scholar), emphasizing the complete incapacity of the opponents.

Key Terms
معجزة
عجز
مأخذ
مجاز
مبالغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mu'jiza directly means 'miracle' without considering its root meaning of showing incapacity.
Correction: The term mu'jiza is derived from 'ajz (incapacity) and specifically denotes an act that exposes the opponent's inability to replicate it; the suffix intensifies this meaning.

Checkpoint
What is the root (ma'khadh) of the word 'mu'jiza' according to the author?
A) Power (qudra)
B) Incapacity ('ajz)
C) Challenge (tahaddi)
D) Sign (aya)

Key Takeaway
The term mu'jiza is rooted in 'ajz (incapacity), metaphorically applied to the prophetic act, and intensified by the هاء suffix to emphasize the complete inability of the opponents to produce its like.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the word 'mu'jiza' (miracle) in relation to the Qur'an, does your mind first go to the power of Allah or to your own utter inability to produce anything like it? In what specific area of your life do you need to feel that deep sense of 'ajz right now?
?
The هاء in mu'jiza emphasizes the complete incapacity of the opponents. Do you truly believe, in your heart of hearts, that you could never produce even a single verse like the Qur'an? Or is there a secret pride that thinks, 'Maybe if I tried...'? Sit with that question and let the answer humble you.
?
This lesson shows that the miracle is called 'mu'jiza' because it displays the helplessness of those who reject it. Have you ever stood before an ayah and felt not awe, but a desire to argue or excuse? What would it take for you to approach the Qur'an not as a critic, but as someone who knows they are powerless before its Author?

Philosophers' Definition of Miracle
Philosophers define a miracle (mu'jiza) concisely.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of a miracle according to the philosophers, including its key components and what it excludes.

Core Idea
A miracle is a divine act that exceeds all natural and psychic power.

Theologians and philosophers sometimes define miracles differently. In this lesson, we examine the philosophical definition, which focuses on the divine action being individual, precise, and surpassing the limits of both natural forces and psychic abilities. This definition is foundational for understanding how philosophers differentiate miracles from ordinary events or extraordinary human feats.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the three key qualifiers: 'individual' (juz'i), 'precise' (muhkam), and 'divine action' (fi'l ilahi). Then notice what it surpasses: natural power and psychic powers.
The philosophical definition is concise; each word carries technical weight.
2
Analyze the components
Break down the definition: a miracle is (1) a particular act (not a general phenomenon), (2) precisely crafted, (3) from God. It exceeds both physical nature and the human psyche's capacities.
3
Contrast with common misconception
Note that philosophers do NOT require a miracle to break God's custom (sunnah). Their definition is purely about surpassing created powers. This distinguishes it from the Ash'ari definition.
The common misunderstanding is to impute 'breaking custom' into this definition.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this definition allows philosophers to classify certain acts as miracles even if they are not violations of natural law, as long as they outstrip all natural and psychic ability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحدها على رأي الفلاسفة: أنها هي الفعل الجزئي المحكم الإلهي الذي يفوت منتهى القوة الطبيعية والقوى النفسانية.
Its definition according to the philosophers: that it is an individual, precise, divine action that surpasses the utmost of natural power and the psychic powers.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage gives the definition of miracle (mu'jiza) according to philosophers. They define it as a particular (juz'i) and perfect (muhkam) divine action that exceeds both natural physical power and psychic (psychological) powers. This highlights the miraculous nature as beyond ordinary human capabilities.

Key Terms
فعل جزئي محكم إلهي
قوة طبيعية
قوى نفسانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that philosophers define a miracle as a violation of the customary course of nature (kharq al-'ada), similar to the Ash'ari definition.
Correction: The philosophical definition does not include the condition of breaking custom. It defines a miracle as any divine action that surpasses all natural and psychic powers, regardless of whether it breaks a pattern.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what does a miracle (mu'jiza) surpass?
A) Only natural power
B) Only psychic power
C) Both natural and psychic powers
D) Divine power

Key Takeaway
The philosophical definition of a miracle is a singular, perfect divine act that exceeds the limits of both nature and the human psyche, without requiring the suspension of natural laws.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If a miracle is defined as surpassing all natural and psychic power, have you ever witnessed something in your life that you considered a miracle? Was it truly beyond all natural explanation, or did you attribute it to a natural cause? How does this definition challenge your understanding of divine intervention?
?
Philosophers define a miracle as a 'divine action' (fi'l ilahi). Do you believe that God still performs such actions today? If so, where do you see them? If not, why do you think miracles ceased? Answer honestly: does your belief align more with the philosophers or with those who expect miracles regularly?
?
The definition excludes breaking custom. How does that affect your view of the miracles in the Quran? Are they still miraculous if they don't break natural laws? How does this make you feel about the role of reason in faith?
